<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with the silicon-containing synthetic polymer <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a>.</div>

.navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_silicon" title="Template:Infobox silicon"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_silicon" title="Template talk:Infobox silicon"><span title="Discuss this <p><b>Silicon</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemistry)" title="Symbol (chemistry)">symbol</a> <b>Si</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 14. It is a hard, brittle crystalline solid with a blue-grey metallic luster, and is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetravalence" title="Tetravalence">tetravalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a>. It is a member of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">group 14</a> in the periodic table: <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> is above it; and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flerovium" title="Flerovium">flerovium</a> are below it. It is relatively unreactive.
</p><p>Because of its high chemical affinity for oxygen, it was not until 1823 that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> was first able to prepare it and characterize it in pure form. Its <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> form a family of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anions</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>. Its melting and boiling points of 1414 °C and 3265 °C, respectively, are the second highest among all the metalloids and nonmetals, being surpassed only by <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>.
</p><p>Silicon is the eighth <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">most common element</a> in the universe by mass, but very rarely occurs as the pure element in the Earth's crust. It is widely distributed in space in cosmic <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dusts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetoids" title="Planetoids">planetoids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> as various forms of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (silica) or <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>. More than 90% of the Earth's crust is composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a>, making silicon the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">second most abundant element</a> in the Earth's crust (about 28% by mass), after <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.
</p><p>Most silicon is used commercially without being separated, often with very little processing of the natural minerals. Such use includes industrial construction with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clays" title="Clays">clays</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica_sand" title="Silica sand">silica sand</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone" title="Stone">stone</a>. Silicates are used in <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> for <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a>, and mixed with silica sand and <a href="/wiki/Gravel" title="Gravel">gravel</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> for walkways, foundations, and roads. They are also used in whiteware <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>, and in traditional <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a>-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda-lime_glass" title="Soda-lime glass">soda-lime glass</a> and many other specialty <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glasses</a>. Silicon compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> are used as abrasives and components of high-strength ceramics. Silicon is the basis of the widely used synthetic polymers called <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicones</a>.
</p><p>The late 20th century to early 21st century has been described as the Silicon Age (also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_Age" title="Digital Age">Digital Age</a> or <a href="/wiki/Information_Age" title="Information Age">Information Age</a>) because of the large impact that elemental silicon has on the modern world economy. The small portion of very highly purified elemental silicon used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductor_electronics" title="Semiconductor electronics">semiconductor electronics</a> (&lt;10%<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>) is essential to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transistors" title="Transistors">transistors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuit</a> chips used in most modern technology such as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computers</a>. In 2019, 32.4% of the semiconductor market segment was for networks and communications devices, and the semiconductors industry is projected to reach $726.73 billion by 2027. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon is an essential element in biology. Only traces are required by most animals, but some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_sponges" title="Sea sponges">sea sponges</a> and microorganisms, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatoms" title="Diatoms">diatoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiolaria" title="Radiolaria">radiolaria</a>, secrete skeletal structures made of silica. Silica is deposited in many plant tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cutter_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cutter-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Owing to the abundance of silicon in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>, natural silicon-based materials have been used for thousands of years. Silicon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_crystal" title="Rock crystal">rock crystals</a> were familiar to various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_civilizations" title="Ancient civilizations">ancient civilizations</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic Egyptians</a> who used it for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beads" title="Beads">beads</a> and small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vases" title="Vases">vases</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient Chinese</a>. <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">Glass</a> containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> was manufactured by the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> since at least 1500 BC, as well as by the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a>. Natural <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> compounds were also used in various types of <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">mortar</a> for construction of early human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwellings" title="Dwellings">dwellings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="469" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg/170px-J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg/255px-J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg/340px-J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> discovered the silicon element in 1823.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1787, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> suspected that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> might be an oxide of a fundamental <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_affinity" title="Chemical affinity">chemical affinity</a> of silicon for oxygen is high enough that he had no means to reduce the oxide and isolate the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328-14">[14]</a></sup> After an attempt to isolate silicon in 1808, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Humphry_Davy" title="Sir Humphry Davy">Sir Humphry Davy</a> proposed the name "silicium" for silicon, from the Latin <i>silex</i>, <i>silicis</i> for flint, and adding the "-ium" ending because he believed it to be a metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Most other languages use transliterated forms of Davy's name, sometimes adapted to local phonology (e.g. <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Silizium</i>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> <i>silisyum</i>, <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a> <i>silici</i>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> <i>Սիլիցիում</i> or <i>Silitzioum</i>). A few others use instead a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> of the Latin root (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <i>кремний</i>, from <i>кремень</i> "flint"; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>πυρίτιο</i> from <i>πυρ</i> "fire"; <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> <i>pii</i> from <i>piikivi</i> "flint", <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a> <i>křemík</i> from <i>křemen</i> "quartz", "flint").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay-Lussac" title="Gay-Lussac">Gay-Lussac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jacques_Th%C3%A9nard" title="Louis Jacques Thénard">Thénard</a> are thought to have prepared impure <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">amorphous silicon</a> in 1811, through the heating of recently isolated <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> metal with <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrafluoride" title="Silicon tetrafluoride">silicon tetrafluoride</a>, but they did not purify and characterize the product, nor identify it as a new element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Silicon was given its present name in 1817 by Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Thomson_(chemist)" title="Thomas Thomson (chemist)">Thomas Thomson</a>. He retained part of Davy's name but added "-on" because he believed that silicon was a <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetal</a> similar to <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1824, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> prepared amorphous silicon using approximately the same method as Gay-Lussac (reducing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluorosilicate" title="Potassium fluorosilicate">potassium fluorosilicate</a> with molten potassium metal), but purifying the product to a brown powder by repeatedly washing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> As a result, he is usually given credit for the element's discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The same year, Berzelius became the first to prepare <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrachloride" title="Silicon tetrachloride">silicon tetrachloride</a>; <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrafluoride" title="Silicon tetrafluoride">silicon tetrafluoride</a> had already been prepared long before in 1771 by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> by dissolving silica in <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon in its more common crystalline form was not prepared until 31 years later, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Etienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" title="Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Deville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> By <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolyzing</a> a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> containing approximately 10% silicon, he was able to obtain a slightly impure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">allotrope</a> of silicon in 1854.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Later, more cost-effective methods have been developed to isolate several allotrope forms, the most recent being <a href="/wiki/Silicene" title="Silicene">silicene</a> in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aufray2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aufray2010-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lalmi2010_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lalmi2010-26">[26]</a></sup> Meanwhile, research on the chemistry of silicon continued; <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> discovered the first volatile hydrides of silicon, synthesising <a href="/wiki/Trichlorosilane" title="Trichlorosilane">trichlorosilane</a> in 1857 and <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">silane</a> itself in 1858, but a detailed investigation of the <a href="/wiki/Silanes" title="Silanes">silanes</a> was only carried out in the early 20th century by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Stock" title="Alfred Stock">Alfred Stock</a>, despite early speculation on the matter dating as far back as the beginnings of synthetic organic chemistry in the 1830s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Similarly, the first <a href="/wiki/Organosilicon_compound" title="Organosilicon compound">organosilicon compound</a>, tetraethylsilane, was synthesised by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Friedel" title="Charles Friedel">Charles Friedel</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Crafts" title="James Crafts">James Crafts</a> in 1863, but detailed characterisation of organosilicon chemistry was only done in the early 20th century by <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Kipping" title="Frederic Kipping">Frederic Kipping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting in the 1920s, the work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" title="William Lawrence Bragg">William Lawrence Bragg</a> on <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> successfully elucidated the compositions of the silicates, which had previously been known from <a href="/wiki/Analytical_chemistry" title="Analytical chemistry">analytical chemistry</a> but had not yet been understood, together with <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a>'s development of <a href="/wiki/Crystal_chemistry" title="Crystal chemistry">crystal chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Victor_Goldschmidt" title="Victor Goldschmidt">Victor Goldschmidt</a>'s development of <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemistry</a>. The middle of the 20th century saw the development of the chemistry and industrial use of <a href="/wiki/Siloxane" title="Siloxane">siloxanes</a> and the growing use of <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elastomer" title="Elastomer">elastomers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a>. In the late 20th century, the complexity of the crystal chemistry of <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicides</a> was mapped, along with the <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_physics" title="Solid-state physics">solid-state physics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">doped</a> <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicon_semiconductors">Silicon semiconductors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Silicon semiconductors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductor_devices" title="Semiconductor devices">semiconductor devices</a> did not use silicon, but used <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a>, including German <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Braun" title="Ferdinand Braun">Ferdinand Braun</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crystal_detector" title="Crystal detector">crystal detector</a> in 1874 and Indian physicist <a href="/wiki/Jagadish_Chandra_Bose" title="Jagadish Chandra Bose">Jagadish Chandra Bose</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> crystal detector in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerhistory-timeline_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-computerhistory-timeline-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerhistory-1901_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-computerhistory-1901-30">[30]</a></sup> The first silicon semiconductor device was a silicon radio crystal detector, developed by American engineer <a href="/wiki/Greenleaf_Whittier_Pickard" title="Greenleaf Whittier Pickard">Greenleaf Whittier Pickard</a> in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerhistory-1901_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-computerhistory-1901-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1940, <a href="/wiki/Russell_Ohl" title="Russell Ohl">Russell Ohl</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/P%E2%80%93n_junction" title="P–n junction">p–n junction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">photovoltaic effects</a> in silicon. In 1941, techniques for producing high-purity <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_crystal" title="Silicon crystal">silicon crystals</a> were developed for <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> detector crystals during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-computerhistory-timeline_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-computerhistory-timeline-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1947, physicist <a href="/wiki/William_Shockley" title="William Shockley">William Shockley</a> theorized a <a href="/wiki/Field-effect_transistor" title="Field-effect transistor">field-effect amplifier</a> made from germanium and silicon, but he failed to build a working device, before eventually working with germanium instead. The first working transistor was a <a href="/wiki/Point-contact_transistor" title="Point-contact transistor">point-contact transistor</a> built by <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_Brattain" title="Walter Brattain">Walter Brattain</a> later that year while working under Shockley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1954, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_chemist" title="Physical chemist">physical chemist</a> <a href="/wiki/Morris_Tanenbaum" title="Morris Tanenbaum">Morris Tanenbaum</a> fabricated the first silicon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junction_transistor" title="Junction transistor">junction transistor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1955, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Frosch" title="Carl Frosch">Carl Frosch</a> and Lincoln Derick at Bell Labs accidentally discovered that <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (SiO<sub>2</sub>) could be grown on silicon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bassett22_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett22-33">[33]</a></sup> and they later proposed this could mask silicon surfaces during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diffusion_processes" title="Diffusion processes">diffusion processes</a> in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saxena_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saxena-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicon_Age">Silicon Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Silicon Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOSFET_Structure.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="613" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/MOSFET_Structure.png/220px-MOSFET_Structure.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/MOSFET_Structure.png/330px-MOSFET_Structure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/MOSFET_Structure.png/440px-MOSFET_Structure.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOSFET_Structure.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">MOSFET</a>, also known as the MOS transistor, is the key component of the Silicon Age. It was invented by <a href="/wiki/Mohamed_M._Atalla" title="Mohamed M. Atalla">Mohamed M. Atalla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dawon_Kahng" title="Dawon Kahng">Dawon Kahng</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a> in 1959.</div></div></div>
<p>The "Silicon Age" refers to the late 20th century to early 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dabrowski_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dabrowski-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siffert_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siffert-37">[37]</a></sup> This is due to silicon being the dominant material of the Silicon Age (also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_Age" title="Digital Age">Digital Age</a> or <a href="/wiki/Information_Age" title="Information Age">Information Age</a>), similar to how the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> were defined by the dominant materials during their respective <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">ages of civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because silicon is an important element in high-technology semiconductor devices, many places in the world bear its name. For example, <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara_Valley" title="Santa Clara Valley">Santa Clara Valley</a> in California acquired the nickname <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>, as the element is the base material in the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_industry" title="Semiconductor industry">semiconductor industry</a> there. Since then, many other places have been dubbed similarly, including <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Wadi" title="Silicon Wadi">Silicon Wadi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Forest" title="Silicon Forest">Silicon Forest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Hills" title="Silicon Hills">Silicon Hills</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Slopes" title="Silicon Slopes">Silicon Slopes</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City,_Utah" title="Salt Lake City, Utah">Salt Lake City, Utah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Saxony" title="Silicon Saxony">Silicon Saxony</a> in Germany, <a href="/wiki/Bangalore#Economy" title="Bangalore">Silicon Valley</a> in India, <a href="/wiki/Mexicali#Silicon_Border" title="Mexicali">Silicon Border</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexicali,_Mexico" title="Mexicali, Mexico">Mexicali, Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Fen" title="Silicon Fen">Silicon Fen</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge,_England" title="Cambridge, England">Cambridge, England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Street_Roundabout#Silicon_Roundabout" title="Old Street Roundabout">Silicon Roundabout</a> in London, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Glen" title="Silicon Glen">Silicon Glen</a> in Scotland, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Gorge" title="Silicon Gorge">Silicon Gorge</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bristol,_England" title="Bristol, England">Bristol, England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Alley" title="Silicon Alley">Silicon Alley</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York,_New_York" title="New York, New York">New York, New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Beach" title="Silicon Beach">Silicon Beach</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_and_atomic">Physical and atomic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical and atomic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png/180px-Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png/270px-Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png/360px-Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silicon-unit-cell-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon crystallizes in a <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">cubic crystal structure</a> by forming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid orbitals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A silicon atom has fourteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a>. In the ground state, they are arranged in the electron configuration [Ne]3s<sup>2</sup>3p<sup>2</sup>. Of these, four are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>, occupying the 3s orbital and two of the 3p orbitals. Like the other members of its group, the lighter <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and the heavier <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, it has the same number of valence electrons as valence orbitals: hence, it can complete its <a href="/wiki/Octet_rule" title="Octet rule">octet</a> and obtain the stable <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> configuration of <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> by forming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid orbitals</a>, forming tetrahedral SiX<sub>4</sub> derivatives where the central silicon atom shares an electron pair with each of the four atoms it is bonded to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King0_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-King0-40">[40]</a></sup> The first four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionisation_energy" title="Ionisation energy">ionisation energies</a> of silicon are 786.3, 1576.5, 3228.3, and 4354.4 kJ/mol respectively; these figures are high enough to preclude the possibility of simple cationic chemistry for the element. Following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_trend" title="Periodic trend">periodic trends</a>, its single-bond covalent radius of 117.6 pm is intermediate between those of carbon (77.2 pm) and germanium (122.3 pm). The hexacoordinate ionic radius of silicon may be considered to be 40 pm, although this must be taken as a purely notional figure given the lack of a simple Si<sup>4+</sup> cation in reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997372_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997372-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical">Electrical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electrical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At standard temperature and pressure, silicon is a shiny <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> with a bluish-grey metallic lustre; as typical for semiconductors, its resistivity drops as temperature rises. This arises because silicon has a small energy gap (<a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">band gap</a>) between its highest occupied energy levels (the valence band) and the lowest unoccupied ones (the conduction band). The <a href="/wiki/Fermi_level" title="Fermi level">Fermi level</a> is about halfway between the <a href="/wiki/Valence_and_conduction_bands" title="Valence and conduction bands">valence and conduction bands</a> and is the energy at which a state is as likely to be occupied by an electron as not. Hence pure silicon is effectively an insulator at room temperature. However, <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">doping</a> silicon with a <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogen</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a> introduces one extra electron per dopant and these may then be excited into the conduction band either thermally or photolytically, creating an <a href="/wiki/Extrinsic_semiconductor#N-type_semiconductors" title="Extrinsic semiconductor">n-type semiconductor</a>. Similarly, doping silicon with a <a href="/wiki/Boron_group" title="Boron group">group 13 element</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> results in the introduction of acceptor levels that trap electrons that may be excited from the filled valence band, creating a <a href="/wiki/Extrinsic_semiconductor#P-type_semiconductors" title="Extrinsic semiconductor">p-type semiconductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>  Joining n-type silicon to p-type silicon creates a <a href="/wiki/P%E2%80%93n_junction" title="P–n junction">p–n junction</a> with a common Fermi level; electrons flow from n to p, while holes flow from p to n, creating a voltage drop. This p–n junction thus acts as a <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">diode</a> that can rectify alternating current that allows current to pass more easily one way than the other. A <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> is an n–p–n junction, with a thin layer of weakly p-type silicon between two n-type regions. Biasing the emitter through a small forward voltage and the collector through a large reverse voltage allows the transistor to act as a <a href="/wiki/Triode" title="Triode">triode</a> amplifier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crystal_structure">Crystal structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Crystal structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Silicon crystallises in a giant covalent structure at standard conditions, specifically in a <a href="/wiki/Diamond_cubic" title="Diamond cubic">diamond cubic</a> lattice (<a href="/wiki/Category:Crystals_in_space_group_227" title="Category:Crystals in space group 227">space group 227</a>). It thus has a high melting point of 1414 °C, as a lot of energy is required to break the strong covalent bonds and melt the solid. Upon melting silicon contracts as the long-range tetrahedral network of bonds breaks up and the voids in that network are filled in, similar to water ice when hydrogen bonds are broken upon melting. It does not have any thermodynamically stable allotropes at standard pressure, but several other crystal structures are known at higher pressures. The general trend is one of increasing <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> with pressure, culminating in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_close-packed" title="Hexagonal close-packed">hexagonal close-packed</a> allotrope at about 40 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigapascal" title="Gigapascal">gigapascals</a> known as Si–VII (the standard modification being Si–I). An allotrope called BC8 (or bc8), having a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centred_cubic" title="Body-centred cubic">body-centred cubic</a> lattice with eight atoms per primitive unit cell (<a href="/wiki/Category:Crystals_in_space_group_206" title="Category:Crystals in space group 206">space group 206</a>), can be created at high pressure and remains metastable at low pressure. Its properties have been studied in detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon boils at 3265 °C: this, while high, is still lower than the temperature at which its lighter congener <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> sublimes (3642 °C) and silicon similarly has a lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_vaporisation" title="Heat of vaporisation">heat of vaporisation</a> than carbon, consistent with the fact that the Si–Si bond is weaker than the C–C bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is also possible to construct <a href="/wiki/Silicene" title="Silicene">silicene</a> layers analogous to <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aufray2010_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aufray2010-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lalmi2010_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lalmi2010-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_silicon" title="Isotopes of silicon">Isotopes of silicon</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring silicon is composed of three stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, <sup>28</sup>Si (92.23%), <sup>29</sup>Si (4.67%), and <sup>30</sup>Si (3.10%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup> Out of these, only <sup>29</sup>Si is of use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR" title="NMR">NMR</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EPR_spectroscopy" title="EPR spectroscopy">EPR spectroscopy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> as it is the only one with a nuclear spin (<i>I</i> =<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup> All three are produced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernovae" title="Type Ia supernovae">Type Ia supernovae</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> through the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-burning_process" title="Oxygen-burning process">oxygen-burning process</a>, with <sup>28</sup>Si being made as part of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_process" title="Alpha process">alpha process</a> and hence the most abundant. The fusion of <sup>28</sup>Si with alpha particles by <a href="/wiki/Photodisintegration" title="Photodisintegration">photodisintegration</a> rearrangement in stars is known as the <a href="/wiki/Silicon-burning_process" title="Silicon-burning process">silicon-burning process</a>; it is the last stage of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> before the rapid collapse and violent explosion of the star in question in a <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">type II supernova</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Twenty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotopes" title="Radioisotopes">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, the two stablest being <sup>32</sup>Si with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of about 150 years, and <sup>31</sup>Si with a half-life of 2.62 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup> All the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are less than seven seconds, and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than one tenth of a second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup> Silicon has one known <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">nuclear isomer</a>, <sup>34m</sup>Si, with a half-life less than 210 nanoseconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup> <sup>32</sup>Si undergoes low-energy <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> to <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus-32" title="Phosphorus-32"><sup>32</sup>P</a> and then stable <sup>32</sup><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a>. <sup>31</sup>Si may be produced by the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> of natural silicon and is thus useful for quantitative analysis; it can be easily detected by its characteristic beta decay to stable <sup>31</sup><a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">P</a>, in which the emitted electron carries up to 1.48 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">MeV</a> of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The known isotopes of silicon range in <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> from 22 to 44.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup> The most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_mode" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> of the isotopes with mass numbers lower than the three stable isotopes is <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>, primarily forming aluminium isotopes (13 protons) as <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup> The most common decay mode for the heavier unstable isotopes is beta decay, primarily forming phosphorus isotopes (15 protons) as decay products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon can enter the oceans through groundwater and riverine transport. Large fluxes of groundwater input have an isotopic composition which is distinct from riverine silicon inputs. Isotopic variations in groundwater and riverine transports contribute to variations in oceanic <sup>30</sup>Si values. Currently, there are substantial differences in the isotopic values of deep water in the world's ocean basins. Between the Atlantic and Pacific oceans, there is a deep water <sup>30</sup>Si gradient of greater than 0.3 parts per thousand. <sup>30</sup>Si is most commonly associated with productivity in the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_compounds">Chemistry and compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Chemistry and compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-top:0; margin-left:1em; text-align:center; font-size:10pt; line-height:11pt; width:25%;">
