<p>Crystalline bulk silicon is rather inert, but becomes more reactive at high temperatures. Like its neighbour aluminium, silicon forms a thin, continuous surface layer of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (SiO<sub>2</sub>) that protects the metal from oxidation. Thus silicon does not measurably react with the air below 900 °C, but formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vitreous_lustre" title="Vitreous lustre">vitreous</a> dioxide rapidly increases between 950 °C and 1160 °C and when 1400 °C is reached, atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> also reacts to give the nitrides SiN and Si<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. Silicon reacts with gaseous <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> at 600 °C and gaseous <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> at 1000 °C. This oxide layer nevertheless does not prevent reaction with the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>; <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> attacks silicon vigorously at room temperature, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> does so at about 300 °C, and <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> at about 500 °C. Silicon does not react with most aqueous acids, but is oxidised and complexed by <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> mixtures containing either <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Hexafluorosilicic_acid" title="Hexafluorosilicic acid">hexafluorosilicates</a>. It readily dissolves in hot aqueous alkali to form <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> At high temperatures, silicon also reacts with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_halide" title="Alkyl halide">alkyl halides</a>; this reaction may be catalysed by <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> to directly synthesise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organosilicon" title="Organosilicon">organosilicon</a> chlorides as precursors to <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> polymers. Upon melting, silicon becomes extremely reactive, alloying with most metals to form <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicides</a>, and reducing most metal oxides because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_formation" title="Heat of formation">heat of formation</a> of silicon dioxide is so large. In fact, molten silicon reacts virtually with every known kind of crucible material (excepting its own oxide, SiO<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 13">: 13 </span></sup> This happens due to Silicon high binding forces for the light elements and to its high dissolving power for most elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 13">: 13 </span></sup> As a result, containers for liquid silicon must be made of <a href="/wiki/Refractory" title="Refractory">refractory</a>, unreactive materials such as <a href="/wiki/Zirconium_dioxide" title="Zirconium dioxide">zirconium dioxide</a> or group 4, 5, and 6 borides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tetrahedral coordination is a major structural motif in silicon chemistry just as it is for carbon chemistry. However, the 3p subshell is rather more diffuse than the 2p subshell and does not hybridise so well with the 3s subshell. As a result, the chemistry of silicon and its heavier congeners shows significant differences from that of carbon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaupp_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaupp-53">[53]</a></sup> and thus octahedral coordination is also significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of silicon (1.90) is much less than that of carbon (2.55), because the valence electrons of silicon are further from the nucleus than those of carbon and hence experience smaller electrostatic forces of attraction from the nucleus. The poor overlap of 3p orbitals also results in a much lower tendency toward <a href="/wiki/Catenation" title="Catenation">catenation</a> (formation of Si–Si bonds) for silicon than for carbon, due to the concomitant weakening of the Si–Si bond compared to the C–C bond:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King43_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-King43-54">[54]</a></sup> the average Si–Si bond energy is approximately 226 kJ/mol, compared to a value of 356 kJ/mol for the C–C bond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997374_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997374-55">[55]</a></sup> This results in multiply bonded silicon compounds generally being much less stable than their carbon counterparts, an example of the <a href="/wiki/Double_bond_rule" title="Double bond rule">double bond rule</a>. On the other hand, the presence of radial nodes in the 3p orbitals of silicon suggests the possibility of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypervalence" title="Hypervalence">hypervalence</a>, as seen in five and six-coordinate derivatives of silicon such as <span class="chemf nowrap">SiX<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">SiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King43_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-King43-54">[54]</a></sup> Lastly, because of the increasing energy gap between the valence s and p orbitals as the group is descended, the divalent state grows in importance from carbon to lead, so that a few unstable divalent compounds are known for silicon; this lowering of the main oxidation state, in tandem with increasing atomic radii, results in an increase of metallic character down the group. Silicon already shows some incipient metallic behavior, particularly in the behavior of its oxide compounds and its reaction with acids as well as bases (though this takes some effort), and is hence often referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> rather than a nonmetal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King43_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-King43-54">[54]</a></sup> However, metallicity does not become clear in group 14 until germanium and dominant until tin, with the growing importance of the lower +2 oxidation state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997328-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon shows clear differences from carbon. For example, <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a> has very few analogies with silicon chemistry, while <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> minerals have a structural complexity unseen in <a href="/wiki/Oxocarbon" title="Oxocarbon">oxocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997327–328_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997327–328-57">[57]</a></sup> Silicon tends to resemble germanium far more than it does carbon, and this resemblance is enhanced by the <a href="/wiki/D-block_contraction" title="D-block contraction">d-block contraction</a>, resulting in the size of the germanium atom being much closer to that of the silicon atom than periodic trends would predict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359–361_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359–361-58">[58]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there are still some differences because of the growing importance of the divalent state in germanium compared to silicon, which result in germanium being significantly more metallic than silicon. Additionally, the lower Ge–O bond strength compared to the <a href="/wiki/Silicon%E2%80%93oxygen_bond" title="Silicon–oxygen bond">Si–O bond</a> strength results in the absence of "germanone" polymers that would be analogous to <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> polymers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997374_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997374-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicides">Silicides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Silicides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">Silicide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Binary_compounds_of_silicon" title="Binary compounds of silicon">Binary compounds of silicon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="427" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg/400px-Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg/600px-Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg/800px-Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagramme_binaire_Fe_Si_analyse_thermique_30.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phase diagram of the Fe–Si system</div></div></div>
<p>Many metal <a href="/wiki/Silicide" title="Silicide">silicides</a> are known, most of which have formulae that cannot be explained through simple appeals to <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">valence</a>: their bonding ranges from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metallic_bond" title="Metallic bond">metallic</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" title="Ionic bond">ionic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a>. Some known stoichiometries are M<sub>6</sub>Si, M<sub>5</sub>Si, M<sub>4</sub>Si, M<sub>15</sub>Si<sub>4</sub>, M<sub>3</sub>Si, M<sub>5</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>, M<sub>2</sub>Si, M<sub>5</sub>Si<sub>3</sub>, M<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>, MSi, M<sub>2</sub>Si<sub>3</sub>, MSi<sub>2</sub>, MSi<sub>3</sub>, and MSi<sub>6</sub>. They are structurally more similar to the <a href="/wiki/Boride" title="Boride">borides</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a>, in keeping with the <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_relationship" title="Diagonal relationship">diagonal relationship</a> between <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and silicon, although the larger size of silicon than boron means that exact structural analogies are few and far between. The heats of formation of the silicides are usually similar to those of the borides and carbides of the same elements, but they usually melt at lower temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997335-337_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997335-337-59">[59]</a></sup> Silicides are known for all stable elements in groups 1–10, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>: in particular, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and the transition metals of groups 4–10 show the widest range of stoichiometries. Except for <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, the metals in groups 11–15 do not form silicides. Instead, most form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" title="Eutectic mixture">eutectic mixtures</a>, although the heaviest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a> <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> are completely immiscible with liquid silicon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Usually, silicides are prepared by direct reaction of the elements. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a> react with silicon or silicon oxide to give silicides. Nevertheless, even with these highly electropositive elements true silicon anions are not obtainable, and most of these compounds are semiconductors. For example, the alkali metal silicides <span class="chemf nowrap">(M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)</span> contain pyramidal tricoordinate silicon in the <span class="chemf nowrap">Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anion, isoelectronic with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_phosphorus" title="White phosphorus">white phosphorus</a>, P<sub>4</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King45_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-King45-60">[60]</a></sup> Metal-rich silicides tend to have isolated silicon atoms (e. g. Cu<sub>5</sub>Si); with increasing silicon content, catenation increases, resulting in isolated clusters of two (e. g. U<sub>3</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>) or four silicon atoms (e. g. [K<sup>+</sup>]<sub>4</sub>[Si<sub>4</sub>]<sup>4−</sup>) at first, followed by chains (e. g. CaSi), layers (e. g. CaSi<sub>2</sub>), or three-dimensional networks of silicon atoms spanning space (e. g. α-ThSi<sub>2</sub>) as the silicon content rises even higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The silicides of the group 1 and 2 metals usually are more reactive than the transition metal silicides. The latter usually do not react with aqueous reagents, except for <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a>; however, they do react with much more aggressive reagents such as liquid <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxide</a>, or gaseous fluorine or <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> when red-hot. The pre-transition metal silicides instead readily react with water and aqueous acids, usually producing hydrogen or silanes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Na<sub>2</sub>Si + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O → Na<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>Mg<sub>2</sub>Si + 2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → 2 MgSO<sub>4</sub> + SiH<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Products often vary with the stoichiometry of the silicide reactant. For example, Ca<sub>2</sub>Si is polar and non-conducting and has the anti-PbCl<sub>2</sub> structure with single isolated silicon atoms, and reacts with water to produce <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a>, hydrated silicon dioxide, and hydrogen gas. CaSi with its zigzag chains of silicon atoms instead reacts to give silanes and polymeric SiH<sub>2</sub>, while CaSi<sub>2</sub> with its puckered layers of silicon atoms does not react with water, but will react with dilute hydrochloric acid: the product is a yellow polymeric solid with stoichiometry Si<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331_42-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997331-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silanes">Silanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Silanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silanes" title="Silanes">Silanes</a></div>
<p>Speculation on silicon hydride chemistry started in the 1830s, contemporary with the development of synthetic <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">Silane</a> itself, as well as <a href="/wiki/Trichlorosilane" title="Trichlorosilane">trichlorosilane</a>, were first synthesised by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Heinrich_Buff&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Heinrich Buff (page does not exist)">Heinrich Buff</a> in 1857 by reacting aluminium–silicon alloys with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, and characterised as SiH<sub>4</sub> and SiHCl<sub>3</sub> by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Friedel" title="Charles Friedel">Charles Friedel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Ladenburg" title="Albert Ladenburg">Albert Ladenburg</a> in 1867. <a href="/wiki/Disilane" title="Disilane">Disilane</a> (Si<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>) followed in 1902, when it was first made by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Moissan" title="Henri Moissan">Henri Moissan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Smiles" title="Samuel Smiles">Samuel Smiles</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Protonolysis" title="Protonolysis">protonolysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_silicide" title="Magnesium silicide">magnesium silicides</a>. Further investigation had to wait until 1916 because of the great reactivity and thermal instability of the silanes; it was then that <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Stock" title="Alfred Stock">Alfred Stock</a> began to study silicon hydrides in earnest with new greaseless vacuum techniques, as they were found as contaminants of his focus, the <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">boron hydrides</a>. The names <i>silanes</i> and <i>boranes</i> are his, based on analogy with the <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The Moissan and Smiles method of preparation of silanes and silane derivatives via protonolysis of metal silicides is still used, although the yield is lowered by the hydrolysis of the products that occurs simultaneously, so that the preferred route today is to treat substituted silanes with hydride reducing agents such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a> in etheric solutions at low temperatures. Direct reaction of HX or RX with silicon, possibly with a catalyst such as copper, is also a viable method of producing substituted silanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The silanes comprise a <a href="/wiki/Homologous_series" title="Homologous series">homologous series</a> of silicon hydrides with a general formula of Si<sub><i>n</i></sub>H<sub>2<i>n</i> + 2</sub>. They are all strong <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agents</a>. Unbranched and branched chains are known up to <i>n</i>=8, and the cycles Si<sub>5</sub>H<sub>10</sub> and Si<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub> are also known. The first two, silane and disilane, are colourless gases; the heavier members of the series are volatile liquids. All silanes are very reactive and catch fire or explode spontaneously in air. They become less thermally stable with room temperature, so that only silane is indefinitely stable at room temperature, although disilane does not decompose very quickly (only 2.5% of a sample decomposes after the passage of eight months).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup> They decompose to form polymeric <a href="/wiki/Polysilicon_hydride" title="Polysilicon hydride">polysilicon hydride</a> and hydrogen gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> As expected from the difference in atomic weight, the silanes are less volatile than the corresponding alkanes and boranes, but more so than the corresponding germanes. They are much more reactive than the corresponding alkanes, because of the larger radius of silicon compared to carbon facilitating <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophilic attack</a> at the silicon, the greater polarity of the Si–H bond compared to the C–H bond, and the ability of silicon to expand its octet and hence form adducts and lower the reaction's <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silane <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> gives polymeric species and finally elemental silicon and hydrogen; indeed ultrapure silicon is commercially produced by the pyrolysis of silane. While the thermal decomposition of alkanes starts by the breaking of a C–H or C–C bond and the formation of radical intermediates, polysilanes decompose by eliminating <a href="/wiki/Silylene" title="Silylene">silylenes</a> :SiH<sub>2</sub> or :SiHR, as the activation energy of this process (~210 kJ/mol) is much less than the Si–Si and Si–H bond energies. While pure silanes do not react with pure water or dilute acids, traces of alkali catalyse immediate hydrolysis to hydrated silicon dioxide. If the reaction is carried out in <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, controlled solvolysis results in the products SiH<sub>2</sub>(OMe)<sub>2</sub>, SiH(OMe)<sub>3</sub>, and Si(OMe)<sub>4</sub>. The Si–H bond also adds to <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a>, a reaction which proceeds slowly and speeds up with increasing substitution of the silane involved. At 450 °C, silane participates in an <a href="/wiki/Addition_reaction" title="Addition reaction">addition reaction</a> with <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, as well as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring-opening_reaction" title="Ring-opening reaction">ring-opening reaction</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a>. Direct reaction of the silanes with chlorine or bromine results in explosions at room temperature, but the reaction of silane with bromine at −80 °C is controlled and yields bromosilane and dibromosilane. The monohalosilanes may be formed by reacting silane with the appropriate <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_halide" title="Hydrogen halide">hydrogen halide</a> with an Al<sub>2</sub>X<sub>6</sub> catalyst, or by reacting silane with a solid <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> halide in a heated flow reactor:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiH<sub>4</sub> + 2 AgCl <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;">260 °C</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;">→</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"> </span></span></span> SiH<sub>3</sub>Cl + HCl + 2 Ag</dd></dl>
<p>Among the derivatives of silane, <a href="/wiki/Iodosilane" title="Iodosilane">iodosilane</a> (SiH<sub>3</sub>I) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Potassium_silanide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Potassium silanide (page does not exist)">potassium silanide</a> (KSiH<sub>3</sub>) are very useful synthetic intermediates in the production of more complicated silicon-containing compounds: the latter is a colourless crystalline ionic solid containing K<sup>+</sup> cations and <span class="chemf nowrap">SiH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> anions in the <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">NaCl</a> structure, and is made by the reduction of silane by <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King47_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-King47-65">[65]</a></sup> Additionally, the reactive hypervalent species <span class="chemf nowrap">SiH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> is also known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup> With suitable organic substituents it is possible to produce stable polysilanes: they have surprisingly high electric conductivities, arising from <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">sigma</a> delocalisation of the electrons in the chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Silicon and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> readily react with all four stable halogens, forming the colourless, reactive, and volatile silicon tetrahalides<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrafluoride" title="Silicon tetrafluoride">Silicon tetrafluoride</a> also may be made by fluorinating the other silicon halides, and is produced by the attack of <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> on glass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King48_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-King48-68">[68]</a></sup> Heating two different tetrahalides together also produces a random mixture of mixed halides, which may also be produced by halogen exchange reactions. The melting and boiling points of these species usually rise with increasing atomic weight, though there are many exceptions: for example, the melting and boiling points drop as one passes from SiFBr<sub>3</sub> through SiFClBr<sub>2</sub> to SiFCl<sub>2</sub>Br. The shift from the hypoelectronic elements in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_13_element" title="Group 13 element">Group 13</a> and earlier to the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">Group 14 elements</a> is illustrated by the change from an infinite ionic structure in <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_fluoride" title="Aluminium fluoride">aluminium fluoride</a> to a lattice of simple covalent silicon tetrafluoride molecules, as dictated by the lower electronegativity of aluminium than silicon, the stoichiometry (the +4 oxidation state being too high for true ionicity), and the smaller size of the silicon atom compared to the aluminium atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrachloride" title="Silicon tetrachloride">Silicon tetrachloride</a> is manufactured on a huge scale as a precursor to the production of pure silicon, silicon dioxide, and some silicon <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340-67">[67]</a></sup> The silicon tetrahalides hydrolyse readily in water, unlike the carbon tetrahalides, again because of the larger size of the silicon atom rendering it more open to nucleophilic attack and the ability of the silicon atom to expand its octet which carbon lacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King48_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-King48-68">[68]</a></sup> The reaction of silicon fluoride with excess <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">hydrofluoric acid</a> produces the octahedral <a href="/wiki/Hexafluorosilicic_acid" title="Hexafluorosilicic acid">hexafluorosilicate</a> anion <span class="chemf nowrap">SiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King48_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-King48-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Analogous to the silanes, halopolysilanes Si<sub><i>n</i></sub>X<sub>2<i>n</i> + 2</sub> also are known. While catenation in carbon compounds is maximised in the hydrogen compounds rather than the halides, the opposite is true for silicon, so that the halopolysilanes are known up to at least Si<sub>14</sub>F<sub>30</sub>, Si<sub>6</sub>Cl<sub>14</sub>, and Si<sub>4</sub>Br<sub>10</sub>. A suggested explanation for this phenomenon is the compensation for the electron loss of silicon to the more electronegative halogen atoms by <a href="/wiki/Pi_backbonding" title="Pi backbonding">pi backbonding</a> from the filled p<sub>π</sub> orbitals on the halogen atoms to the empty d<sub>π</sub> orbitals on silicon: this is similar to the situation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl_complex" title="Metal carbonyl complex">metal carbonyl complexes</a> and explains their stability. These halopolysilanes may be produced by <a href="/wiki/Comproportionation" title="Comproportionation">comproportionation</a> of silicon tetrahalides with elemental silicon, or by condensation of lighter halopolysilanes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trimethylammonium" title="Trimethylammonium">trimethylammonium</a> being a useful catalyst for this reaction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997340-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silica">Silica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Silica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">Silicon dioxide</a> (SiO<sub>2</sub>), also known as silica, is one of the best-studied compounds, second only to <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a>. Twelve different crystal modifications of silica are known, the most common being α-<a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, a major constituent of many rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a>. It also is known to occur in a pure form as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_crystal" title="Rock crystal">rock crystal</a>; impure forms are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rose_quartz" title="Rose quartz">rose quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smoky_quartz" title="Smoky quartz">smoky quartz</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morion_(mineral)" title="Morion (mineral)">morion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amethyst" title="Amethyst">amethyst</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citrine_quartz" title="Citrine quartz">citrine</a>. Some poorly crystalline forms of quartz are also known, such as <a href="/wiki/Chalcedony" title="Chalcedony">chalcedony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrysoprase" title="Chrysoprase">chrysoprase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">carnelian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agate" title="Agate">agate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onyx" title="Onyx">onyx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jasper" title="Jasper">jasper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heliotrope_(mineral)" title="Heliotrope (mineral)">heliotrope</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a>. Other modifications of silicon dioxide are known in some other minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Tridymite" title="Tridymite">tridymite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cristobalite" title="Cristobalite">cristobalite</a>, as well as the much less common <a href="/wiki/Coesite" title="Coesite">coesite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stishovite" title="Stishovite">stishovite</a>. Biologically generated forms are also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kieselguhr" title="Kieselguhr">kieselguhr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vitrification" title="Vitrification">Vitreous</a> silicon dioxide is known as <a href="/wiki/Tektite" title="Tektite">tektites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a>, and rarely as <a href="/wiki/Lechatelierite" title="Lechatelierite">lechatelierite</a>. Some synthetic forms are known as <a href="/wiki/Keatite" title="Keatite">keatite</a>. <a href="/wiki/Opal" title="Opal">Opals</a> are composed of complicated crystalline aggregates of partially hydrated silicon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342-347_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342-347-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Most crystalline forms of silica are made of infinite arrangements of {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra (with Si at the center) connected at their corners, with each oxygen atom linked to two silicon atoms. In the thermodynamically stable room-temperature form, α-quartz, these tetrahedra are linked in intertwined helical chains with two different Si–O distances (159.7 and 161.7 pm) with a Si–O–Si angle of 144°. These helices can be either left- or right-handed, so that individual α-quartz crystals are optically active. At 537 °C, this transforms quickly and reversibly into the similar β-quartz, with a change of the Si–O–Si angle to 155° but a retention of handedness. Further heating to 867 °C results in another reversible phase transition to β-tridymite, in which some Si–O bonds are broken to allow for the arrangement of the {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra into a more open and less dense hexagonal structure. This transition is slow and hence tridymite occurs as a metastable mineral even below this transition temperature; when cooled to about 120 °C it quickly and reversibly transforms by slight displacements of individual silicon and oxygen atoms to α-tridymite, similarly to the transition from α-quartz to β-quartz. β-tridymite slowly transforms to cubic β-cristobalite at about 1470 °C, which once again exists metastably below this transition temperature and transforms at 200–280 °C to α-cristobalite via small atomic displacements. β-cristobalite melts at 1713 °C; the freezing of silica from the melt is quite slow and <a href="/wiki/Vitrification" title="Vitrification">vitrification</a>, or the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, is likely to occur instead. In vitreous silica, the {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra remain corner-connected, but the symmetry and periodicity of the crystalline forms are lost. Because of the slow conversions between these three forms, it is possible upon rapid heating to melt β-quartz (1550 °C) or β-tridymite (1703 °C). Silica boils at approximately 2800 °C. Other high-pressure forms of silica are known, such as coesite and stishovite: these are known in nature, formed under the shock pressure of a meteorite impact and then rapidly quenched to preserve the crystal structure. Similar melting and cooling of silica occurs following <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> strikes, forming glassy <a href="/wiki/Lechatelierite" title="Lechatelierite">lechatelierite</a>. W-silica is an unstable low-density form involving {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra sharing opposite edges instead of corners, forming parallel chains similarly to <a href="/wiki/Silicon_disulfide" title="Silicon disulfide">silicon disulfide</a> (SiS<sub>2</sub>) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon_diselenide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Silicon diselenide (page does not exist)">silicon diselenide</a> (SiSe<sub>2</sub>): it quickly returns to forming amorphous silica with heat or traces of water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG/220px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG/330px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG/440px-%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BA%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%82%D0%B0_H2SiO3.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Condensed polysilicic acid</div></div></div>
<p>Silica is rather inert chemically. It is not attacked by any acids other than hydrofluoric acid. However, it slowly dissolves in hot concentrated alkalis, and does so rather quickly in fused metal hydroxides or carbonates, to give metal silicates. Among the elements, it is attacked only by fluorine at room temperature to form silicon tetrafluoride: hydrogen and carbon also react, but require temperatures over 1000 °C to do so. Silica nevertheless reacts with many metal and <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloid</a> oxides to form a wide variety of compounds important in the glass and ceramic industries above all, but also have many other uses: for example, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_silicate" title="Sodium silicate">sodium silicate</a> is often used in detergents due to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffer_(chemistry)" title="Buffer (chemistry)">buffering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponifying</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emulsification" title="Emulsification">emulsifying</a> properties<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicic_acids">Silicic acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Silicic acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adding water to silica drops its melting point by around 800 °C due to the breaking of the structure by replacing Si–O–Si linkages with terminating Si–OH groups. Increasing water concentration results in the formation of hydrated <a href="/wiki/Silica_gel" title="Silica gel">silica gels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloidal</a> silica dispersions. Many hydrates and <a href="/wiki/Silicic_acid" title="Silicic acid">silicic acids</a> exist in the most dilute of aqueous solutions, but these are rather insoluble and quickly precipitate and condense and cross-link to form various polysilicic acids of variable combinations following the formula [SiO<sub><i>x</i></sub>(OH)<sub>4−2<i>x</i></sub>]<sub><i>n</i></sub>, similar to the behaviour of <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, among other elements. Hence, although some simple silicic acids have been identified in dilute solutions, such as <a href="/wiki/Orthosilicic_acid" title="Orthosilicic acid">orthosilicic acid</a> Si(OH)<sub>4</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Metasilicic_acid" title="Metasilicic acid">metasilicic acid</a> SiO(OH)<sub>2</sub>, none of these are likely to exist in the solid state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997342-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicate_minerals">Silicate minerals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Silicate minerals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
