<p>About 95% of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crustal</a> rocks are made of silica or silicate and <a href="/wiki/Aluminosilicate" title="Aluminosilicate">aluminosilicate</a> minerals, as reflected in oxygen, silicon, and aluminium being the three most common elements in the crust (in that order).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347-71">[71]</a></sup> Measured by mass, silicon makes up 27.7% of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Pure silicon crystals are very rarely found in nature, but notable exceptions are crystals as large as to 0.3 mm across found during sampling gases from the <a href="/wiki/Medvezhya" title="Medvezhya">Kudriavy</a> volcano on <a href="/wiki/Iturup" title="Iturup">Iturup</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KorzhinskyTkachenko1995_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-KorzhinskyTkachenko1995-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicate and aluminosilicate minerals have many different structures and varying stoichiometry, but they may be classified following some general principles. Tetrahedral {SiO<sub>4</sub>} units are common to almost all these compounds, either as discrete structures, or combined into larger units by the sharing of corner oxygen atoms. These may be divided into <i>neso</i>-silicates (discrete {SiO<sub>4</sub>} units) sharing no oxygen atoms, <i>soro</i>-silicates (discrete {Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>} units) sharing one, <i>cyclo</i>-silicates (closed ring structures) and <i>ino</i>-silicates (continuous chain or ribbon structures) both sharing two, <i>phyllo</i>-silicates (continuous sheets) sharing three, and <i>tecto</i>-silicates (continuous three-dimensional frameworks) sharing four. The lattice of oxygen atoms that results is usually close-packed, or close to it, with the charge being balanced by other cations in various different polyhedral sites according to size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347-359_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347-359-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Orthosilicate" title="Orthosilicate">orthosilicates</a> M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">II</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span> (M = Be, Mg, Mn, Fe, Zn) and ZrSiO<sub>4</sub> are <i>neso</i>-silicates. <span class="chemf nowrap">Be<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenacite" title="Phenacite">phenacite</a>) is unusual as both Be<sup>II</sup> and Si<sup>IV</sup> occupy tetrahedral four-coordinated sites; the other divalent cations instead occupy six-coordinated octahedral sites and often isomorphously replace each other as in <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, (Mg,Fe,Mn)<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>. <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">Zircon</a>, ZrSiO<sub>4</sub>, demands eight-coordination of the Zr<sup>IV</sup> cations due to stoichiometry and because of their larger ionic radius (84 pm). Also significant are the <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnets</a>, [M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">II</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>M<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">III</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>], in which the divalent cations (e.g. Ca, Mg, Fe) are eight-coordinated and the trivalent ones are six-coordinated (e.g. Al, Cr, Fe). Regular coordination is not always present: for example, it is not found in Ca<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>, which mixes six- and eight-coordinate sites for Ca<sup>II</sup>. <i>Soro</i>-silicates, involving discrete double or triple tetrahedral units, are quite rare: metasilicates involving cyclic "[(SiO<sub>3</sub>)<sub><i>n</i></sub>]<sup>2<i>n</i>−</sup>" units of corner-abutting tetrahedra forming a polygonal ring are also known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chain metasilicates, {SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>}<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">∞</sub></span>, form by corner-sharing of an indefinite chain of linked {SiO<sub>4</sub>} tetrahedra. Many differences arise due to the differing repeat distances of conformation across the line of tetrahedra. A repeat distance of two is most common, as in most <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a> minerals, but repeat distances of one, three, four, five, six, seven, nine, and twelve are also known. These chains may then link across each other to form double chains and ribbons, as in the <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> minerals, involving repeated chains of cyclic tetrahedron rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1420" data-file-width="1876" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/220px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/330px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/440px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical zeolite structure</div></div></div>
<p>Layer silicates, such as the clay minerals and the <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">micas</a>, are very common, and often are formed by horizontal cross-linking of metasilicate chains or planar condensation of smaller units. An example is <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a> [Al<sub>2</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>]; in many of these minerals cation and anion replacement is common, so that for example tetrahedral Si<sup>IV</sup> may be replaced by Al<sup>III</sup>, octahedral Al<sup>III</sup> by Mg<sup>II</sup>, and OH<sup>−</sup> by F<sup>−</sup>. Three-dimensional framework aluminosilicates are structurally very complex; they may be conceived of as starting from the SiO<sub>2</sub> structure, but having replaced up to one-half of the Si<sup>IV</sup> atoms with Al<sup>III</sup>, they require more cations to be included in the structure to balance charge. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspars</a> (the most abundant minerals on the Earth), <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarines</a>. Many feldspars can be thought of as forming part of the ternary system NaAlSi<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>–KAlSi<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>–CaAl<sub>2</sub>Si<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. Their lattice is destroyed by high pressure prompting Al<sup>III</sup> to undergo six-coordination rather than four-coordination, and this reaction destroying feldspars may be a reason for the <a href="/wiki/Mohorovi%C4%8Di%C4%87_discontinuity" title="Mohorovičić discontinuity">Mohorovičić discontinuity</a>, which would imply that the crust and mantle have the same chemical composition, but different lattices, although this is not a universally held view. Zeolites have many polyhedral cavities in their frameworks (<a href="/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron" title="Truncated cuboctahedron">truncated cuboctahedra</a> being most common, but other polyhedra also are known as zeolite cavities), allowing them to include loosely bound molecules such as water in their structure. Ultramarines alternate silicon and aluminium atoms and include a variety of other anions such as Cl<sup>−</sup>, <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, but are otherwise similar to the feldspars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997347-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_inorganic_compounds">Other inorganic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other inorganic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_disulfide" title="Silicon disulfide">Silicon disulfide</a> (SiS<sub>2</sub>) is formed by burning silicon in gaseous sulfur at 100 °C; sublimation of the resulting compound in nitrogen results in white, flexible long fibers reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> with a structure similar to W-silica. This melts at 1090 °C and sublimes at 1250 °C; at high temperature and pressure this transforms to a crystal structure analogous to cristobalite. However, SiS<sub>2</sub> lacks the variety of structures of SiO<sub>2</sub>, and quickly hydrolyses to silica and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. It is also ammonolysed quickly and completely by liquid <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> as follows to form an <a href="/wiki/Imide" title="Imide">imide</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359–361_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359–361-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiS<sub>2</sub> + 4 NH<sub>3</sub> → Si(NH)<sub>2</sub> + 2 NH<sub>4</sub>SH</dd></dl>
<p>It reacts with the sulfides of sodium, magnesium, aluminium, and iron to form metal <a href="/wiki/Thiosilicate" title="Thiosilicate">thiosilicates</a>: reaction with <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> results in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraethylsilicate" title="Tetraethylsilicate">tetraethylsilicate</a> Si(OEt)<sub>4</sub> and hydrogen sulfide. Ethylsilicate is useful as its controlled hydrolysis produces adhesive or film-like forms of silica. Reacting hydrogen sulfide with silicon tetrahalides yields silicon thiohalides such as S(SiCl)<sub>3</sub>, cyclic Cl<sub>2</sub>Si(μ-S)<sub>2</sub>SiCl<sub>2</sub>, and crystalline (SiSCl<sub>2</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. Despite the <a href="/wiki/Double_bond_rule" title="Double bond rule">double bond rule</a>, stable organosilanethiones RR'Si=S have been made thanks to the stabilising mechanism of intermolecular coordination via an <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_nitride" title="Silicon nitride">Silicon nitride</a>, Si<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, may be formed by directly reacting silicon with nitrogen above 1300 °C, but a more economical means of production is by heating silica and coke in a stream of nitrogen and hydrogen gas at 1500 °C. It would make a promising <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> if not for the difficulty of working with and sintering it: chemically, it is near-totally inert, and even above 1000 °C it keeps its strength, shape, and continues to be resistant to wear and corrosion. It is very hard (9 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_hardness_scale" title="Mohs hardness scale">Mohs hardness scale</a>), dissociates only at 1900 °C at 1 atm, and is quite dense (density 3.185 g/cm<sup>3</sup>), because of its compact structure similar to that of phenacite (<span class="chemf nowrap">Be<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). A similar refractory material is Si<sub>2</sub>N<sub>2</sub>O, formed by heating silicon and silica at 1450 °C in an argon stream containing 5% nitrogen gas, involving 4-coordinate silicon and 3-coordinate nitrogen alternating in puckered hexagonal tilings interlinked by non-linear Si–O–Si linkages to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reacting silyl halides with ammonia or alkylammonia derivatives in the gaseous phase or in ethanolic solution produces various volatile silylamides, which are silicon analogues of the <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 SiH<sub>3</sub>Cl + 4 NH<sub>3</sub> → N(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 3 NH<sub>4</sub>Cl</dd>
<dd>SiH<sub>3</sub>Br + 2 Me<sub>2</sub>NH → SiH<sub>3</sub>NMe<sub>2</sub> + Me<sub>2</sub>NH<sub>2</sub>Br</dd>
<dd>4 SiH<sub>3</sub>I + 5 N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> → (SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>NN(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 4 N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>I</dd></dl>
<p>Many such compounds have been prepared, the only known restriction being that the nitrogen is always tertiary, and species containing the SiH–NH group are unstable at room temperature. The stoichiometry around the nitrogen atom in compounds such as N(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>is planar, which has been attributed to a p<sub>π</sub>–d<sub>π</sub> interaction between a lone pair on nitrogen and an empty d<sub>π</sub> orbital on silicon. Similarly, trisilylamines are weaker as ligands than their carbon analogues, the tertiary amines, although substitution of some SiH<sub>3</sub> groups by CH<sub>3</sub> groups mitigates this weakness. For example, N(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub> does not form an <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adduct</a> with <a href="/wiki/Borane" title="Borane">BH<sub>3</sub></a> at all, while MeN(SiH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and Me<sub>2</sub>NSiH<sub>3</sub> form adducts at low temperatures that decompose upon warming. Some silicon analogues of <a href="/wiki/Imine" title="Imine">imines</a>, with a Si=N double bond, are known: the first found was Bu<sup><i>t</i></sup><sub>2</sub>Si=N–SiBu<sup><i>t</i></sup><sub>3</sub>, which was discovered in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997359-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SiC_p1390066.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="1419" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/220px-SiC_p1390066.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/330px-SiC_p1390066.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SiC_p1390066.jpg/440px-SiC_p1390066.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SiC_p1390066.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon carbide</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">Silicon carbide</a> (SiC) was first made by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Goodrich_Acheson" title="Edward Goodrich Acheson">Edward Goodrich Acheson</a> in 1891, who named it carborundum to reference its intermediate hardness and abrasive power between <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> (an allotrope of carbon) and <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a>). He soon founded a company to manufacture it, and today about one million tonnes are produced each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997334_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997334-77">[77]</a></sup> Silicon carbide exists in about 250 crystalline forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The polymorphism of SiC is characterized by a large family of similar crystalline structures called polytypes. They are variations of the same chemical compound that are identical in two dimensions and differ in the third. Thus they can be viewed as layers stacked in a certain sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jap_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-jap-79">[79]</a></sup> It is made industrially by reduction of quartz sand with excess coke or anthracite at 2000–2500 °C in an electric furnace:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997334_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997334-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiO<sub>2</sub> + 2 C → Si + 2 CO</dd>
<dd>Si + C → SiC</dd></dl>
<p>It is the most thermally stable binary silicon compound, only decomposing through loss of silicon starting from around 2700 °C. It is resistant to most aqueous acids, <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a> being an exception. It forms a protective layer of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> on the surface and hence only oxidises appreciably in air above 1000 °C; removal of this layer by molten hydroxides or carbonates leads to quick oxidation. Silicon carbide is rapidly attacked by chlorine gas, which forms SiCl<sub>4</sub> and carbon at 100 °C and SiCl<sub>4</sub> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">CCl<sub>4</sub></a> at 1000 °C. It is mostly used as an abrasive and a refractory material, as it is chemically stable and very strong, and it fractures to form a very sharp cutting edge. It is also useful as an intrinsic semiconductor, as well as an extrinsic semiconductor upon being doped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997334_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997334-77">[77]</a></sup> In its diamond-like behavior it serves as an illustration of the chemical similarity between carbon and silicon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organosilicon_compounds">Organosilicon compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Organosilicon compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organosilicon" title="Organosilicon">Organosilicon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="1057" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png/400px-HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png/600px-HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png/800px-HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HydrosilylationTriphenylsilylhydride.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A hydrosilylation reaction, in which Si–H is added to an unsaturated substrate</div></div></div>
<p>Because the Si–C <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bond</a> is close in strength to the C–C bond, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organosilicon" title="Organosilicon">organosilicon</a> compounds tend to be markedly thermally and chemically stable. For example, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tetraphenylsilane&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tetraphenylsilane (page does not exist)">tetraphenylsilane</a> (SiPh<sub>4</sub>) may be distilled in air even at its boiling point of 428 °C, and so may its substituted derivatives Ph<sub>3</sub>SiCl and Ph<sub>2</sub>SiCl<sub>2</sub>, which boil at 378 °C and 305 °C respectively. Furthermore, since carbon and silicon are chemical congeners, organosilicon chemistry shows some significant similarities with carbon chemistry, for example in the propensity of such compounds for catenation and forming multiple bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361-80">[80]</a></sup> However, significant differences also arise: since silicon is more electropositive than carbon, bonds to more electronegative elements are generally stronger with silicon than with carbon, and vice versa. Thus the Si–F bond is significantly stronger than even the <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93fluorine_bond" title="Carbon–fluorine bond">C–F bond</a> and is one of the strongest single bonds, while the Si–H bond is much weaker than the C–H bond and is readily broken. Furthermore, the ability of silicon to expand its octet is not shared by carbon, and hence some organosilicon reactions have no organic analogues. For example, nucleophilic attack on silicon does not proceed by the <a href="/wiki/SN2_reaction" title="SN2 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>2</a> or <a href="/wiki/SN1_reaction" title="SN1 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>1</a> processes, but instead goes through a negatively charged true pentacoordinate intermediate and appears like a substitution at a hindered tertiary atom. This works for silicon, unlike for carbon, because the long Si–C bonds reduce the steric hindrance and the d-orbital of silicon is geometrically unconstrained for nucleophilic attack, unlike for example a C–O σ* antibonding orbital. Nevertheless, despite these differences, the mechanism is still often called "S<sub>N</sub>2 at silicon" for simplicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayden668_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayden668-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most useful silicon-containing groups is <a href="/wiki/Trimethylsilyl" title="Trimethylsilyl">trimethylsilyl</a>, Me<sub>3</sub>Si–. The Si–C bond connecting it to the rest of the molecule is reasonably strong, allowing it to remain while the rest of the molecule undergoes reactions, but is not so strong that it cannot be removed specifically when needed, for example by the <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> ion, which is a very weak nucleophile for carbon compounds but a very strong one for organosilicon compounds. It may be compared to acidic <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>; while trisilylmethyl is removed by hard nucleophiles instead of bases, both removals usually promote elimination. As a general rule, while saturated carbon is best attacked by nucleophiles that are neutral compounds, those based on nonmetals far down on the periodic table (e.g. sulfur, <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>), or even both, silicon is best attacked by charged nucleophiles, particularly those involving such highly electronegative nonmetals as oxygen, fluorine, or chlorine. For example, enolates react at the carbon in <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">haloalkanes</a>, but at the oxygen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silyl" title="Silyl">silyl</a> chlorides; and when trimethylsilyl is removed from an organic molecule using hydroxide as a nucleophile, the product of the reaction is not the silanol as one would expect from using carbon chemistry as an analogy, because the siloxide is strongly nucleophilic and attacks the original molecule to yield the <a href="/wiki/Silyl_ether" title="Silyl ether">silyl ether</a> <a href="/wiki/Hexamethyldisiloxane" title="Hexamethyldisiloxane">hexamethyldisiloxane</a>, (Me<sub>3</sub>Si)<sub>2</sub>O. Conversely, while the S<sub>N</sub>2 reaction is mostly unaffected by the presence of a partial positive charge (δ+) at the carbon, the analogous "S<sub>N</sub>2" reaction at silicon is so affected. Thus, for example, the silyl <a href="/wiki/Triflate" title="Triflate">triflates</a> are so electrophilic that they react 10<sup>8</sup> to 10<sup>9</sup> times faster than silyl chlorides with oxygen-containing nucleophiles. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trimethylsilyl_triflate" title="Trimethylsilyl triflate">Trimethylsilyl triflate</a> is in particular a very good <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> and is used to convert <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyl" title="Carbonyl">carbonyl</a> compounds to <a href="/wiki/Acetal" title="Acetal">acetals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silyl_enol_ether" title="Silyl enol ether">silyl enol ethers</a>, reacting them together analogously to the <a href="/wiki/Aldol_reaction" title="Aldol reaction">aldol reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayden668_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clayden668-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Si–C bonds are commonly formed in three ways. In the laboratory, preparation is often carried out in small quantities by reacting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrachlorosilane" title="Tetrachlorosilane">tetrachlorosilane</a> (silicon tetrachloride) with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organolithium" title="Organolithium">organolithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organoaluminium" title="Organoaluminium">organoaluminium</a> reagents, or by catalytic addition of Si–H across C=C double bonds. The second route has the drawback of not being applicable to the most important silanes, the methyl and phenyl silanes. Organosilanes are made industrially by directly reacting alkyl or aryl halides with silicon with 10% by weight metallic <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> as a catalyst. Standard organic reactions suffice to produce many derivatives; the resulting organosilanes are often significantly more reactive than their carbon congeners, readily undergoing hydrolysis, ammonolysis, alcoholysis, and condensation to form cyclic oligomers or linear polymers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silicone_polymers">Silicone polymers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Silicone polymers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">Silicone</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PmdsStructure.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="1217" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/PmdsStructure.png/220px-PmdsStructure.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/PmdsStructure.png/330px-PmdsStructure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/PmdsStructure.png/440px-PmdsStructure.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PmdsStructure.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Polydimethylsiloxane" title="Polydimethylsiloxane">polydimethylsiloxane</a>, the principal component of silicones</div></div></div>
<p>The word "silicone" was first used by Frederic Kipping in 1901. He invented the word to illustrate the similarity of chemical formulae between Ph<sub>2</sub>SiO and <a href="/wiki/Benzophenone" title="Benzophenone">benzophenone</a>, Ph<sub>2</sub>CO, although he also stressed the lack of chemical resemblance due to the polymeric structure of Ph<sub>2</sub>SiO, which is not shared by Ph<sub>2</sub>CO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicones may be considered analogous to mineral silicates, in which the methyl groups of the silicones correspond to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isoelectronic" title="Isoelectronic">isoelectronic</a> O<sup>−</sup> of the silicates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361-80">[80]</a></sup> They are quite stable to extreme temperatures, oxidation, and water, and have useful dielectric, antistick, and antifoam properties. Furthermore, they are resistant over long periods of time to ultraviolet radiation and weathering, and are inert physiologically. They are fairly unreactive, but do react with concentrated solutions bearing the hydroxide ion and fluorinating agents, and occasionally, may even be used as mild reagents for selective syntheses. For example, (Me<sub>3</sub>Si)<sub>2</sub>O is valuable for the preparation of derivatives of <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> oxyhalides, converting a <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_hexachloride" title="Tungsten hexachloride">tungsten hexachloride</a> suspension in <a href="/wiki/Dichloroethane" title="Dichloroethane">dichloroethane</a> solution quantitatively to WOCl<sub>4</sub> in under an hour at room temperature, and then to yellow WO<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub> at 100 °C in light petroleum at a yield of 95% overnight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361_80-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997361-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="327" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg/170px-Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg/255px-Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olivine-gem7-10a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Olivine</div></div></div>
<p>Silicon is the eighth most abundant element in the universe, coming after <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>. These abundances are not replicated well on Earth due to substantial separation of the elements taking place during the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Silicon makes up 27.2% of the Earth's crust by weight, second only to oxygen at 45.5%, with which it always is associated in nature. Further fractionation took place in the formation of the Earth by <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">planetary differentiation</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_core" title="Earth's core">Earth's core</a>, which makes up 31.5% of the mass of the Earth, has approximate composition Fe<sub>25</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>S<sub>3</sub>; the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> makes up 68.1% of the Earth's mass and is composed mostly of denser oxides and silicates, an example being <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, (Mg,Fe)<sub>2</sub>SiO<sub>4</sub>; while the lighter siliceous minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminosilicate" title="Aluminosilicate">aluminosilicates</a> rise to the surface and form the crust, making up 0.4% of the Earth's mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997329_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997329-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The crystallisation of <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a> from magma depends on a number of factors; among them are the chemical composition of the magma, the cooling rate, and some properties of the individual minerals to be formed, such as <a href="/wiki/Lattice_energy" title="Lattice energy">lattice energy</a>, melting point, and complexity of their crystal structure. As magma is cooled, <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> appears first, followed by <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibole" title="Amphibole">amphibole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biotite" title="Biotite">biotite</a> mica, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthoclase_feldspar" title="Orthoclase feldspar">orthoclase feldspar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscovite_mica" title="Muscovite mica">muscovite mica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a>, and finally, hydrothermal minerals. This sequence shows a trend toward increasingly complex silicate units with cooling, and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> anions in addition to oxides. Many metals may substitute for silicon. After these igneous rocks undergo <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a>, transport, and deposition, <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> like clay, shale, and sandstone are formed. <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">Metamorphism</a> also may occur at high temperatures and pressures, creating an even vaster variety of minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997329_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997329-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are four sources for silicon fluxes into the ocean include chemical weathering of continental rocks, river transport, dissolution of continental terrigenous silicates, and through the reaction between submarine basalts and hydrothermal fluid which release dissolved silicon. All four of these fluxes are interconnected in the ocean's biogeochemical cycle as they all were initially formed from the weathering of Earth's crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_Ocean_Silica_Cycle_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Ocean_Silica_Cycle-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Approximately 300-900 megatonnes of Aeolian dust is deposited into the world's oceans each year. Of that value, 80-240 megatonnes are in the form of particulate silicon. The total amount of particulate silicon deposition into the ocean is still less than the amount of silicon influx into the ocean via riverine transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atmospheric_transport_of_silicon_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atmospheric_transport_of_silicon-85">[85]</a></sup> Aeolian inputs of particulate lithogenic silicon into the North Atlantic and Western North Pacific oceans are the result of dust settling on the oceans from the Sahara and Gobi Desert, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_Ocean_Silica_Cycle_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Ocean_Silica_Cycle-84">[84]</a></sup> Riverine transports are the major source of silicon influx into the ocean in coastal regions, while silicon deposition in the open ocean is greatly influenced by the settling of Aeolian dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atmospheric_transport_of_silicon_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Atmospheric_transport_of_silicon-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Silicon of 96–99% purity is made by reducing <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">quartzite</a> or sand with highly pure <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>. The reduction is carried out in an <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc_furnace" title="Electric arc furnace">electric arc furnace</a>, with an excess of SiO<sub>2</sub> used to stop <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a> (SiC) from accumulating:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>SiO<sub>2</sub> + 2 C → Si + 2 CO</dd>
<dd>2 SiC + SiO<sub>2</sub> → 3 Si + 2 CO</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ferrosilicon.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1227" data-file-width="1755" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ferrosilicon.JPG/200px-Ferrosilicon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ferrosilicon.JPG/300px-Ferrosilicon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Ferrosilicon.JPG/400px-Ferrosilicon.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ferrosilicon.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ferrosilicon alloy</div></div></div>
<p>This reaction, known as carbothermal reduction of silicon dioxide, usually is conducted in the presence of scrap iron with low amounts of <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Ferrosilicon" title="Ferrosilicon">ferrosilicon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup> Ferrosilicon, an iron-silicon alloy that contains varying ratios of elemental silicon and iron, accounts for about 80% of the world's production of elemental silicon, with China, the leading supplier of elemental silicon, providing 4.6 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (or 2/3rds of world output) of silicon, most of it in the form of ferrosilicon. It is followed by Russia (610,000 t), Norway (330,000 t), Brazil (240,000 t), and the United States (170,000 t).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Ferrosilicon is primarily used by the iron and steel industry (see <a href="#Alloys">below</a>) with primary use as alloying addition in iron or steel and for de-oxidation of steel in integrated steel plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another reaction, sometimes used, is aluminothermal reduction of silicon dioxide, as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann574_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann574-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 SiO<sub>2</sub> + 4 Al → 3 Si + 2 Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Leaching powdered 96–97% pure silicon with water results in ~98.5% pure silicon, which is used in the chemical industry. However, even greater purity is needed for semiconductor applications, and this is produced from the reduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrachlorosilane" title="Tetrachlorosilane">tetrachlorosilane</a> (silicon tetrachloride) or <a href="/wiki/Trichlorosilane" title="Trichlorosilane">trichlorosilane</a>. The former is made by chlorinating scrap silicon and the latter is a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Silicone" title="Silicone">silicone</a> production. These compounds are volatile and hence can be purified by repeated <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a>, followed by reduction to elemental silicon with very pure <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> metal as the reducing agent. The spongy pieces of silicon thus produced are melted and then grown to form cylindrical single crystals, before being purified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zone_refining" title="Zone refining">zone refining</a>. Other routes use the thermal decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">silane</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetraiodosilane" title="Tetraiodosilane">tetraiodosilane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SiI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). Another process used is the reduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_hexafluorosilicate" title="Sodium hexafluorosilicate">sodium hexafluorosilicate</a>, a common waste product of the phosphate fertilizer industry, by metallic <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>: this is highly exothermic and hence requires no outside energy source.
</p><p>Silicon nanostructures can be produced directly from silica using conventional metalothermic processes, or combustion synthesis methods. Such nanostructured silicon materials can be used in various functional applications including the anode of lithium ion batteries, or phorocatalytic applications.
</p><p>Hyperfine silicon is made at a higher purity than almost any other material: <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> production requires impurity levels in silicon crystals less than 1 part per 10<sup>10</sup>, and in special cases impurity levels below 1 part per 10<sup>12</sup> are needed and attained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330_27-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997330-27">[27]</a></sup>
Silicon nanostructures can directly be produced from silica sand using the combustion synthesis approach. Such nanostructured silicon materials can be used as the anode of lithium ion batteries (LIBs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most silicon is used industrially without being purified, and indeed, often with comparatively little processing from its natural form. More than 90% of the Earth's crust is composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a>, which are compounds of silicon and oxygen, often with metallic ions when negatively charged silicate anions require cations to balance the charge. Many of these have direct commercial uses, such as clays, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> sand, and most kinds of building stone. Thus, the vast majority of uses for silicon are as structural compounds, either as the silicate minerals or silica (crude silicon dioxide). Silicates are used in making <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> (made mostly of calcium silicates) which is used in <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">building mortar</a> and modern <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a>, but more importantly, combined with silica sand, and gravel (usually containing silicate minerals such as granite), to make the <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> that is the basis of most of the very largest industrial building projects of the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997356_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997356-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silica is used to make <a href="/wiki/Fire_brick" title="Fire brick">fire brick</a>, a type of ceramic. Silicate minerals are also in whiteware <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>, an important class of products usually containing various types of fired <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> minerals (natural aluminium phyllosilicates). An example is <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>, which is based on the silicate mineral <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">kaolinite</a>. Traditional <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> (silica-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda-lime_glass" title="Soda-lime glass">soda-lime glass</a>) also functions in many of the same ways, and also is used for windows and containers. In addition, specialty silica based <a href="/wiki/Glass_fiber" title="Glass fiber">glass fibers</a> are used for <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">optical fiber</a>, as well as to produce <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a> for structural support and <a href="/wiki/Glass_wool" title="Glass wool">glass wool</a> for thermal insulation.
</p><p>Silicones often are used in <a href="/wiki/Waterproofing" title="Waterproofing">waterproofing</a> treatments, <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">molding</a> compounds, mold-<a href="/wiki/Release_agent" title="Release agent">release agents</a>, mechanical seals, high temperature <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">greases</a> and waxes, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caulking" title="Caulking">caulking</a> compounds. Silicone is also sometimes used in <a href="/wiki/Breast_implant" title="Breast implant">breast implants</a>, contact lenses, <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silly_Putty" title="Silly Putty">Silly Putty</a> was originally made by adding <a href="/wiki/Boric_acid" title="Boric acid">boric acid</a> to <a href="/wiki/Silicone_oil" title="Silicone oil">silicone oil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Other silicon compounds function as high-technology abrasives and new high-strength ceramics based upon <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a>. Silicon is a component of some <a href="/wiki/Superalloy" title="Superalloy">superalloys</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Elemental silicon is added to molten <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> as <a href="/wiki/Ferrosilicon" title="Ferrosilicon">ferrosilicon</a> or silicocalcium alloys to improve performance in casting thin sections and to prevent the formation of <a href="/wiki/Cementite" title="Cementite">cementite</a> where exposed to outside air. The presence of elemental silicon in molten iron acts as a sink for oxygen, so that the steel carbon content, which must be kept within narrow limits for each type of steel, can be more closely controlled. Ferrosilicon production and use is a monitor of the steel industry, and although this form of elemental silicon is grossly impure, it accounts for 80% of the world's use of free silicon. Silicon is an important constituent of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_steel" title="Electrical steel">electrical steel</a>, modifying its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resistivity" title="Resistivity">resistivity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a> properties.
</p><p>The properties of silicon may be used to modify alloys with metals other than iron. "Metallurgical grade" silicon is silicon of 95–99% purity. About 55% of the world consumption of metallurgical purity silicon goes for production of aluminium-silicon alloys (<a href="/wiki/Silumin" title="Silumin">silumin</a> alloys) for aluminium part <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">casts</a>, mainly for use in the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automotive industry</a>. Silicon's importance in aluminium casting is that a significantly high amount (12%) of silicon in aluminium forms a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic_mixture" title="Eutectic mixture">eutectic mixture</a> which solidifies with very little thermal contraction. This greatly reduces tearing and cracks formed from stress as casting alloys cool to solidity. Silicon also significantly improves the hardness and thus wear-resistance of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diecasting_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-diecasting-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">Electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device_fabrication" title="Semiconductor device fabrication">Semiconductor device fabrication</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_industry" title="Semiconductor industry">Semiconductor industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="305" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg/170px-Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg/255px-Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silicon_wafer_with_mirror_finish.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon wafer with mirror finish</div></div></div>
<p>Most elemental silicon produced remains as a ferrosilicon alloy, and only approximately 20% is refined to metallurgical grade purity (a total of 1.3–1.5 million metric tons/year). An estimated 15% of the world production of metallurgical grade silicon is further refined to semiconductor purity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-93">[93]</a></sup> This typically is the "nine-9" or 99.9999999% purity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> nearly defect-free single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystalline" title="Crystalline">crystalline</a> material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Monocrystalline_silicon" title="Monocrystalline silicon">Monocrystalline silicon</a> of such purity is usually produced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Czochralski_process" title="Czochralski process">Czochralski process</a>, and is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Wafer_(electronics)" title="Wafer (electronics)">silicon wafers</a> used in the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_industry" title="Semiconductor industry">semiconductor industry</a>, in electronics, and in some high-cost and high-efficiency <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann590_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann590-96">[96]</a></sup> Pure silicon is an <a href="/wiki/Intrinsic_semiconductor" title="Intrinsic semiconductor">intrinsic semiconductor</a>, which means that unlike metals, it conducts <a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">electron holes</a> and electrons released from atoms by heat; silicon's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> increases with higher temperatures. Pure silicon has too low a conductivity (i.e., too high a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resistivity" title="Resistivity">resistivity</a>) to be used as a circuit element in electronics. In practice, pure silicon is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductors)" title="Doping (semiconductors)">doped</a> with small concentrations of certain other elements, which greatly increase its conductivity and adjust its electrical response by controlling the number and charge (<a href="/wiki/Electron_hole" title="Electron hole">positive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">negative</a>) of activated carriers. Such control is necessary for <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_detector" title="Semiconductor detector">semiconductor detectors</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device" title="Semiconductor device">semiconductor devices</a> used in the computer industry and other technical applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann573_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann573-97">[97]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Silicon_photonics" title="Silicon photonics">silicon photonics</a>, silicon may be used as a continuous wave <a href="/wiki/Raman_laser" title="Raman laser">Raman laser</a> medium to produce coherent light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dekker_2008_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-dekker_2008-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In common <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">integrated circuits</a>, a wafer of monocrystalline silicon serves as a mechanical support for the circuits, which are created by doping and insulated from each other by thin layers of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon oxide</a>, an insulator that is easily produced on Si surfaces by processes of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_oxidation" title="Thermal oxidation">thermal oxidation</a> or <a href="/wiki/LOCOS" title="LOCOS">local oxidation (LOCOS)</a>, which involve exposing the element to oxygen under the proper conditions that can be predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Deal%E2%80%93Grove_model" title="Deal–Grove model">Deal–Grove model</a>. Silicon has become the most popular material for both high power semiconductors and integrated circuits because it can withstand the highest temperatures and greatest electrical activity without suffering <a href="/wiki/Avalanche_breakdown" title="Avalanche breakdown">avalanche breakdown</a> (an <a href="/wiki/Electron_avalanche" title="Electron avalanche">electron avalanche</a> is created when heat produces free electrons and holes, which in turn pass more current, which produces more heat). In addition, the insulating oxide of silicon is not soluble in water, which gives it an advantage over <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> (an element with similar properties which can also be used in semiconductor devices) in certain fabrication techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Monocrystalline silicon is expensive to produce, and is usually justified only in production of integrated circuits, where tiny crystal imperfections can interfere with tiny circuit paths. For other uses, other types of pure silicon may be employed. These include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogenated_amorphous_silicon" title="Hydrogenated amorphous silicon">hydrogenated amorphous silicon</a> and upgraded metallurgical-grade silicon (UMG-Si) used in the production of low-cost, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-area_electronics" title="Large-area electronics">large-area electronics</a> in applications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" title="Liquid crystal display">liquid crystal displays</a> and of large-area, low-cost, thin-film <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_cells" title="Solar cells">solar cells</a>. Such semiconductor grades of silicon are either slightly less pure or polycrystalline rather than monocrystalline, and are produced in comparable quantities as the monocrystalline silicon: 75,000 to 150,000 metric tons per year. The market for the lesser grade is growing more quickly than for monocrystalline silicon. By 2013, polycrystalline silicon production, used mostly in solar cells, was projected to reach 200,000 metric tons per year, while monocrystalline semiconductor grade silicon was expected to remain less than 50,000 tons per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_dots">Quantum dots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Quantum dots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_quantum_dots" title="Silicon quantum dots">Silicon quantum dots</a> are created through the thermal processing of hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Silsesquioxane" title="Silsesquioxane">silsesquioxane</a> into nanocrystals ranging from a few nanometers to a few microns, displaying size dependent <a href="/wiki/Luminescence" title="Luminescence">luminescent</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The nanocrystals display large <a href="/wiki/Stokes_shift" title="Stokes shift">Stokes shifts</a> converting photons in the ultra-violet range to photons in the visible or infrared, depending on the particle size, allowing for applications in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot_display" title="Quantum dot display">quantum dot displays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luminescent_solar_concentrator" title="Luminescent solar concentrator">luminescent solar concentrators</a> due to their limited self absorption. A benefit of using silicon based <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dots</a> over <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a> is the non-toxic, metal-free nature of silicon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
Another application of silicon quantum dots is for sensing of hazardous materials. The sensors take advantage of the luminescent properties of the quantum dots through <a href="/wiki/Quenching_(fluorescence)" title="Quenching (fluorescence)">quenching</a> of the photoluminescence in the presence of the hazardous substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> There are many methods used for hazardous chemical sensing with a few being electron transfer, <a href="/wiki/F%C3%B6rster_resonance_energy_transfer" title="Förster resonance energy transfer">fluorescence resonance energy transfer</a>, and photocurrent generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Electron transfer quenching occurs when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lowest_unoccupied_molecular_orbital" title="Lowest unoccupied molecular orbital">lowest unoccupied molecular orbital</a> (LUMO) is slightly lower in energy than the conduction band of the quantum dot, allowing for the transfer electrons between the two, preventing recombination of the holes and electrons within the nanocrystals. The effect can also be achieved in reverse with a donor molecule having its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highest_occupied_molecular_orbital" title="Highest occupied molecular orbital">highest occupied molecular orbital</a> (HOMO) slightly higher than a valence band edge of the quantum dot, allowing electrons to transfer between them, filling the holes and preventing recombination. Fluorescence resonance energy transfer occurs when a complex forms between the quantum dot and a quencher molecule. The complex will continue to absorb light but when the energy is converted to the ground state it does not release a photon, quenching the material. The third method uses different approach by measuring the <a href="/wiki/Photocurrent" title="Photocurrent">photocurrent</a> emitted by the quantum dots instead of monitoring the photoluminescent display. If the concentration of the desired chemical increases then the photocurrent given off by the nanocrystals will change in response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg/170px-20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg/255px-20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg/340px-20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20110123_185042_Diatom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diatom, enclosed in a silica cell wall</div></div></div>
<p>Although silicon is readily available in the form of <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>, very few organisms use it directly. <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">Diatoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiolaria" title="Radiolaria">radiolaria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siliceous_sponge" title="Siliceous sponge">siliceous sponges</a> use <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_silica" title="Biogenic silica">biogenic silica</a> as a structural material for their skeletons. Some plants accumulate silica in their tissues and require silicon for their growth, for example <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>. Silicon may be taken up by plants as <a href="/wiki/Orthosilicic_acid" title="Orthosilicic acid">orthosilicic acid</a> (also known as monosilicic acid) and transported through the <a href="/wiki/Xylem" title="Xylem">xylem</a>, where it forms amorphous complexes with components of the cell wall. This has been shown to improve cell wall strength and structural integrity in some plants, thereby reducing insect herbivory and pathogenic infections. In certain plants, silicon may also upregulate the production of volatile organic compounds and phytohormones which play a significant role in plant defense mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2002_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2002-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plants_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-plants-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leroy2019_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leroy2019-109">[109]</a></sup> In more advanced plants, the silica <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a> (opal phytoliths) are rigid microscopic bodies occurring in the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abd_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-abd-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plants_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-plants-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horticultural_crop" title="Horticultural crop">horticultural crops</a> are known to protect themselves against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungal_plant_pathogen" title="Fungal plant pathogen">fungal plant pathogens</a> with silica, to such a degree that <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicide</a> application may fail unless accompanied by sufficient silicon nutrition. Silicaceous plant defense molecules activate some <a href="/wiki/Phytoalexin" title="Phytoalexin">phytoalexins</a>, meaning some of them are signalling substances producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acquired_immunity" title="Acquired immunity">acquired immunity</a>. When deprived, some plants will substitute with increased production of other defensive substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plants_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-plants-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Life on Earth is largely composed of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> considers that <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> may have other <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_types_of_biochemistry" title="Hypothetical types of biochemistry">hypothetical types of biochemistry</a>. Silicon is considered an alternative to carbon, as it can create complex and stable molecules with four covalent bonds, required for a <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>-analog, and it is available in large quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_microbial_influences">Marine microbial influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Marine microbial influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diatoms uses silicon in the biogenic silica (BSIO<sub>2</sub>) form,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> which is taken up by the silicon transport protein (SIT) to be predominantly used in the cell wall structure as frustules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>  Silicon enters the ocean in a dissolved form such as silicic acid or silicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-115">[115]</a></sup>  Since diatoms are one of the main users of these forms of silicon, they contribute greatly to the concentration of silicon throughout the ocean. Silicon forms a nutrient-like profile in the ocean due to the diatom productivity in shallow depths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-115">[115]</a></sup> Therefore, less concentration of silicon in the upper ocean and more concentrations of silicon in the deep/lower ocean.
</p><p>Diatom productivity in the upper ocean contribute to the amount of silicon exported to the lower ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>  When diatom cells are lysed in the upper ocean, their nutrients like, iron, zinc, and silicon, are brought to the lower ocean through a process called marine snow. Marine snow involves the downward transfer of particulate organic matter by vertical mixing of dissolved organic matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>  It has been suggested that silicon is considered crucial to diatom productivity and as long as there is silicic acid available for diatoms to utilize, the diatoms can contribute to other important nutrient concentrations in the deep ocean as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In coastal zones, diatoms serve as the major phytoplanktonic organisms and greatly contribute to biogenic silica production. In the open ocean, however, diatoms have a reduced role in global annual silica production. Diatoms in North Atlantic and North Pacific subtropical gyres only contribute about 5-7% of global annual marine silica production. The Southern Ocean produces about one-third of global marine biogenic silica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_World_Ocean_Silica_Cycle_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_World_Ocean_Silica_Cycle-84">[84]</a></sup> The Southern Ocean is referred to as having a "biogeochemical divide"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> since only minuscule amounts of silicon are transported out of this region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_nutrition">Human nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Human nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is some evidence that silicon is important to human health for their nail, hair, bone, and skin tissues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> for example, in studies that demonstrate that premenopausal women with higher dietary silicon intake have higher <a href="/wiki/Bone_density" title="Bone density">bone density</a>, and that silicon supplementation can increase bone volume and density in patients with <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jugdaohsingh2007silicon_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-jugdaohsingh2007silicon-121">[121]</a></sup> Silicon is needed for synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>, of which the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> contains the greatest quantity in the human body,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LoeperLoeper1978_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-LoeperLoeper1978-122">[122]</a></sup> and has been considered an <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">essential element</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niels_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niels-123">[123]</a></sup> nevertheless, it is difficult to prove its essentiality, because silicon is very common, and hence, deficiency symptoms are difficult to reproduce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lipp_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-lipp-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silicon is currently under consideration for elevation to the status of a "plant beneficial substance by the Association of American Plant Food Control Officials (AAPFCO)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presentation_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-presentation-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>People may be exposed to elemental silicon in the workplace by breathing it in, swallowing it, or having contact with the skin or eye. In the latter two cases, silicon poses a slight hazard as an irritant. It is hazardous if inhaled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msds_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-msds-128">[128]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">legal limit</a> for silicon exposure in the workplace as 15 mg/m<sup>3</sup> total exposure and 5 mg/m<sup>3</sup> respiratory exposure over an eight-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 10 mg/m<sup>3</sup> total exposure and 5 mg/m<sup>3</sup> respiratory exposure over an eight-hour workday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Inhalation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystalline" title="Crystalline">crystalline</a> silica dust may lead to <a href="/wiki/Silicosis" title="Silicosis">silicosis</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Occupational_lung_disease" title="Occupational lung disease">occupational lung disease</a> marked by <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> and scarring in the form of <a href="/wiki/Nodule_(medicine)" title="Nodule (medicine)">nodular lesions</a> in the upper lobes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungs" title="Lungs">lungs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlantVoulvoulis2012_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlantVoulvoulis2012-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Cutter-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Cutter_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCutter1978">Cutter, Elizabeth G. London: Edward Arnold. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7131-2639-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7131-2639-6"><bdi>978-0-7131-2639-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Plant+Anatomy.+Part+1+Cells+and+Tissues&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Edward+Arnold&amp;rft.date=1978&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7131-2639-6&amp;rft.aulast=Cutter&amp;rft.aufirst=Elizabeth+G.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASilicon"></span></span>
